			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Rembrandt</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">

				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Dutch artist. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Rembrandt (disambiguation)">Rembrandt (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Rembrandt Harmenszoon van Rijn</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>, -<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɑː/: 'a' in 'father'">ɑː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> <small>Dutch:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Dutch" title="Help:IPA/Dutch">[ˈrɛmbrɑnt ˈɦɑrmə(n)soːn vɑn ˈrɛin]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a1/Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg" class="internal" title="Rembrandtvanrijn.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small>; 15&#160;July 1606<sup id="cite_ref-BY_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BY-1">[1]</a></sup>&#160;– 4&#160;October 1669) was a <a href="/wiki/Dutch_people" title="Dutch people">Dutch</a> <a href="/wiki/Draughtsman" class="mw-redirect" title="Draughtsman">draughtsman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Painter" class="mw-redirect" title="Painter">painter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Printmaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Printmaker">printmaker</a>. An innovative and prolific <a href="/wiki/Old_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Masters">master</a> in three <a href="/wiki/Art_medium" class="mw-redirect" title="Art medium">media</a>, he is generally considered one of the greatest <a href="/wiki/Visual_artists" class="mw-redirect" title="Visual artists">visual artists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_art" title="History of art">history of art</a> and the most important in Dutch art history.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombrich.2C_p._420_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombrich.2C_p._420-3">[3]</a></sup> Unlike most <a href="/wiki/Dutch_masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch masters">Dutch masters</a> of the 17th century, <a href="/wiki/Category:Works_by_Rembrandt" title="Category:Works by Rembrandt">Rembrandt's works</a> depict a wide range of style and subject matter, from <a href="/wiki/Portrait_painting" title="Portrait painting">portraits</a> and self-portraits to landscapes, genre scenes, allegorical and historical scenes, biblical and mythological themes as well as animal studies. His contributions to art came in a period of great wealth and cultural achievement that historians call the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Category:Art_of_the_Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Category:Art of the Dutch Golden Age">Dutch art</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age_painting" title="Dutch Golden Age painting">Dutch painting</a>), although in many ways antithetical to the <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque style</a> that dominated Europe, was extremely prolific and innovative, and gave rise to important new genres. Like many artists of the Dutch Golden Age, such as <a href="/wiki/Jan_Vermeer" class="mw-redirect" title="Jan Vermeer">Jan Vermeer</a> of Delft, Rembrandt was also known as an avid <a href="/wiki/Art_collector" class="mw-redirect" title="Art collector">art collector</a> and <a href="/wiki/Art_dealer" title="Art dealer">dealer</a>.</p>
<p>Rembrandt never went abroad, but he was considerably influenced by the work of the <a href="/wiki/Italian_masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian masters">Italian masters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Netherlandish_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Netherlandish art">Netherlandish artists</a> who had studied in Italy, like <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Lastman" title="Pieter Lastman">Pieter Lastman</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_Caravaggists" class="mw-redirect" title="Utrecht Caravaggists">Utrecht Caravaggists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Flemish_Baroque_painting" title="Flemish Baroque painting">Flemish Baroque</a> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Peter Paul Rubens</a>. Having achieved youthful success as a portrait painter, Rembrandt's later years were marked by personal tragedy and financial hardships. Yet his etchings and paintings were popular throughout his lifetime, his reputation as an artist remained high,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and for twenty years he taught many important Dutch painters.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_203_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_203-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rembrandt's portraits of his contemporaries, self-portraits and illustrations of scenes from the Bible are regarded as his greatest creative triumphs. <a href="/wiki/Self-portraits_by_Rembrandt" title="Self-portraits by Rembrandt">His self-portraits</a> form a unique and intimate biography, in which the artist surveyed himself without vanity and with the utmost sincerity.<sup id="cite_ref-Gombrich.2C_p._420_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gombrich.2C_p._420-3">[3]</a></sup> Rembrandt's foremost contribution in the history of printmaking was his transformation of the etching process from a relatively new reproductive technique into a true <a href="/wiki/Art_form" class="mw-redirect" title="Art form">art form</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Callot" title="Jacques Callot">Jacques Callot</a>. His reputation as the greatest <a href="/wiki/Etcher" class="mw-redirect" title="Etcher">etcher</a> in the history of the medium was established in his lifetime and never questioned since. Few of <a href="/wiki/List_of_paintings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of paintings by Rembrandt">his paintings</a> left the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> whilst he lived, but his <a href="/wiki/Old_master_prints" class="mw-redirect" title="Old master prints">prints</a> were circulated throughout Europe, and his wider reputation was initially based on them alone.</p>
<p>In his works he exhibited knowledge of classical <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a>, which he molded to fit the requirements of his own experience; thus, the depiction of a biblical scene was informed by Rembrandt's knowledge of the specific text, his assimilation of classical composition, and his observations of <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Amsterdam" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish Amsterdam">Jewish population</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_203_204_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_203_204-6">[6]</a></sup> Because of his empathy for the human condition, he has been called "one of the great prophets of civilization".<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_205_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_205-7">[7]</a></sup> The French sculptor <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Rodin" title="Auguste Rodin">Auguste Rodin</a> said, "Compare me with Rembrandt! What sacrilege! With Rembrandt, the colossus of Art! We should prostrate ourselves before Rembrandt and never compare anyone with him!"<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Goya" title="Francisco Goya">Francisco Goya</a>, often considered to be among the last of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Masters">Old Masters</a>, said "I have had three masters: Nature, <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Velázquez">Velázquez</a>, and Rembrandt."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a> wrote, "Rembrandt goes so deep into the mysterious that he says things for which there are no words in any language. It is with justice that they call Rembrandt—magician—that's no easy occupation."<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3020" data-file-height="3827" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Prodigal_Son_in_the_Tavern" class="mw-redirect" title="The Prodigal Son in the Tavern">The Prodigal Son in the Tavern</a></i>, a self-portrait with Saskia, c. 1635</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rembrandt<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Harmenszoon van Rijn was born on 15&#160;July 1606 in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-BY_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BY-1">[1]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a>, now the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. He was the ninth child born to Harmen Gerritszoon van Rijn and Neeltgen Willemsdochter van Zuijtbrouck.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> His family was quite well-to-do; his father was a <a href="/wiki/Miller" title="Miller">miller</a> and his mother was a baker's daughter. Religion is a central theme in Rembrandt's paintings and the religiously fraught period in which he lived makes his faith a matter of interest. His mother was Roman Catholic, and his father belonged to the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Dutch Reformed Church</a>. While his work reveals deep Christian faith, there is no evidence that Rembrandt formally belonged to any church, although he had five of his children christened in Dutch Reformed churches in Amsterdam: four in the Oude Kerk (Old Church) and one, Titus, in the Zuiderkerk (Southern Church).<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a boy he attended <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> school and was enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leiden" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Leiden">University of Leiden</a>, although according to a contemporary he had a greater inclination towards painting; he was soon apprenticed to a Leiden history painter, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_van_Swanenburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacob van Swanenburgh">Jacob van Swanenburgh</a>, with whom he spent three years.<sup id="cite_ref-Houbraken_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houbraken-14">[14]</a></sup> After a brief but important apprenticeship of six months with the painter <a href="/wiki/Pieter_Lastman" title="Pieter Lastman">Pieter Lastman</a> in Amsterdam, Rembrandt stayed a few months with <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Pynas" title="Jacob Pynas">Jacob Pynas</a> and then started his own workshop, though Simon van Leeuwen claimed that <a href="/wiki/Joris_van_Schooten" title="Joris van Schooten">Joris van Schooten</a> taught Rembrandt in Leiden.<sup id="cite_ref-Houbraken_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Houbraken-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Unlike many of his contemporaries who traveled to Italy as part of their artistic training, Rembrandt never left the Dutch Republic during his lifetime.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>He opened a studio in Leiden in 1624 or 1625, which he shared with friend and colleague <a href="/wiki/Jan_Lievens" title="Jan Lievens">Jan Lievens</a>. In 1627, Rembrandt began to accept students, among them <a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Dou" title="Gerrit Dou">Gerrit Dou</a> in 1628.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1629, Rembrandt was discovered by the statesman <a href="/wiki/Constantijn_Huygens" title="Constantijn Huygens">Constantijn Huygens</a> (father of the Dutch mathematician and physicist <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>), who procured for Rembrandt important commissions from the court of The Hague. As a result of this connection, Prince <a href="/wiki/Frederik_Hendrik" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederik Hendrik">Frederik Hendrik</a> continued to purchase paintings from Rembrandt until 1646.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh,_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="278" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh%2C_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5797" data-file-height="7327" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh,_the_Wife_of_the_Artist_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Saskia_van_Uylenburgh" title="Saskia van Uylenburgh">Saskia van Uylenburgh</a>, ca. 1635</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the end of 1631 Rembrandt moved to Amsterdam, then rapidly expanding as the new business capital of the Netherlands, and began to practice as a professional portraitist for the first time, with great success. He initially stayed with an art dealer, <a href="/wiki/Hendrick_van_Uylenburgh" title="Hendrick van Uylenburgh">Hendrick van Uylenburgh</a>, and in 1634, married Hendrick's cousin, <a href="/wiki/Saskia_van_Uylenburgh" title="Saskia van Uylenburgh">Saskia van Uylenburgh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Saskia came from a good family: her father had been a lawyer and the <i>burgemeester</i> (mayor) of <a href="/wiki/Leeuwarden" title="Leeuwarden">Leeuwarden</a>. When Saskia, as the youngest daughter, became an orphan, she lived with an older sister in <a href="/wiki/Het_Bildt" title="Het Bildt">Het Bildt</a>. Rembrandt and Saskia were married in the local church of <a href="/wiki/St._Annaparochie" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Annaparochie">St.&#160;Annaparochie</a> without the presence of Rembrandt's relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In the same year, Rembrandt became a <a href="/wiki/Burgess_(title)" title="Burgess (title)">burgess</a> of Amsterdam and a member of the local guild of painters. He also acquired a number of students, among them <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Bol" title="Ferdinand Bol">Ferdinand Bol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Govert_Flinck" title="Govert Flinck">Govert Flinck</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull-28_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1635, Rembrandt and Saskia moved into their own house, renting in fashionable Nieuwe Doelenstraat. In 1639 they moved to a prominent newly built house (now the <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_House_Museum" title="Rembrandt House Museum">Rembrandt House Museum</a>) in the upscale 'Breestraat' (eng.: 'Broadway'), today known as <a href="/wiki/Jodenbreestraat" title="Jodenbreestraat">Jodenbreestraat</a> (Jodenbreestraat 4,1011 NK Amsterdam-now) in what was becoming the Jewish quarter; then a young upcoming neighborhood. The <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_loan" title="Mortgage loan">mortgage</a> to finance the 13,000 <a href="/wiki/Guilder" title="Guilder">guilder</a> purchase would be a primary cause for later financial difficulties.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull-28_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-23">[23]</a></sup> Rembrandt should easily have been able to pay the house off with his large income, but it appears his spending always kept pace with his income, and he may have made some unsuccessful investments.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> It was there that Rembrandt frequently sought his Jewish neighbors to model for his <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> scenes.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Although they were by now affluent, the couple suffered several personal setbacks; their son Rumbartus died two months after his birth in 1635 and their daughter Cornelia died at just three weeks of age in 1638. In 1640, they had a second daughter, also named Cornelia, who died after living barely over a month. Only their fourth child, <a href="/wiki/Titus_van_Rijn" title="Titus van Rijn">Titus</a>, who was born in 1641, survived into adulthood. Saskia died in 1642 soon after Titus's birth, probably from <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>. Rembrandt's drawings of her on her sick and death bed are among his most moving works.<sup id="cite_ref-Slive-71_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-71-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_(Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_%28Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4602" data-file-height="5470" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_Rembrandts_zoon_Titus_in_monniksdracht_(Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rembrandt's son <a href="/wiki/Titus_as_a_Monk" title="Titus as a Monk">Titus, as a monk</a>, 1660</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During Saskia's illness, <a href="/wiki/Geertje_Dircx" title="Geertje Dircx">Geertje Dircx</a> was hired as Titus' caretaker and nurse and also became Rembrandt's lover. She would later charge Rembrandt with <a href="/wiki/Breach_of_promise" title="Breach of promise">breach of promise</a> (a euphemism for seduction under [breached] promise to marry) and was awarded alimony of 200 guilders a year.<sup id="cite_ref-Bull-28_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bull-28-23">[23]</a></sup> Rembrandt worked to have her committed for twelve years to an asylum or poorhouse (called a "bridewell") at <a href="/wiki/Gouda,_South_Holland" title="Gouda, South Holland">Gouda</a>, after learning she had pawned jewelry he had given her that once belonged to Saskia.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the late 1640s Rembrandt began a relationship with the much younger <a href="/wiki/Hendrickje_Stoffels" title="Hendrickje Stoffels">Hendrickje Stoffels</a>, who had initially been his maid. In 1654 they had a daughter, Cornelia, bringing Hendrickje a summons from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Reformed Church</a> to answer the charge "that she had committed the acts of a whore with Rembrandt the painter". She admitted this and was banned from receiving communion. Rembrandt was not summoned to appear for the Church council because he was not a member of the Reformed Church.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The two were considered legally wed under common law, but Rembrandt had not married Hendrickje. Had he remarried he would have lost access to a trust set up for Titus in Saskia's will.<sup id="cite_ref-Slive-71_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slive-71-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rembrandt lived beyond his means, buying art (including bidding up his own work), prints (often used in his paintings) and rarities, which probably caused a court arrangement to avoid his bankruptcy in 1656, by selling most of his paintings and large collection of antiquities. The sale list survives and gives us a good insight into Rembrandt's collections, which, apart from <a href="/wiki/Old_Master" title="Old Master">Old Master</a> paintings and drawings, included busts of the Roman Emperors, suits of Japanese armor among many objects from Asia, and collections of natural history and minerals. But the prices realized in the sales in 1657 and 1658 were disappointing.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Rembrandt was forced to sell his house and his printing-press and move to more modest accommodation on the Rozengracht in 1660.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The authorities and his creditors were generally accommodating to him, except for the Amsterdam <a href="/wiki/Painters%27_guild" class="mw-redirect" title="Painters' guild">painters' guild</a>, which introduced a new rule that no one in Rembrandt's circumstances could trade as a painter. To get around this, Hendrickje and Titus set up a business as art dealers in 1660, with Rembrandt as an employee.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg" width="220" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Memorial_Marker_Westerkerk_Amsterdam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rembrandt Memorial Marker Westerkerk Amsterdam</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1661 Rembrandt (or rather the new business) was contracted to complete work for the newly built city hall, but only after Govert Flinck, the artist previously commissioned, died without beginning to paint. The resulting work, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conspiracy_of_Claudius_Civilis" title="The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis">The Conspiracy of Claudius Civilis</a></i>, was rejected and returned to the painter; the surviving fragment is only a fraction of the whole work.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It was around this time that Rembrandt took on his last apprentice, <a href="/wiki/Aert_de_Gelder" title="Aert de Gelder">Aert de Gelder</a>. In 1662 he was still fulfilling major commissions for portraits and other works.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_III_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo III de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo&#160;III de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a> came to Amsterdam in 1667, he visited Rembrandt at his house.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rembrandt outlived both Hendrickje, who died in 1663, and Titus, who died in 1668, leaving a baby daughter. He died within a year of his son, on 4&#160;October 1669 in Amsterdam, and was buried as a poor man.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> in an unknown grave in the <i><a href="/wiki/Westerkerk" title="Westerkerk">Westerkerk</a></i>. It was in a numbered 'kerkgraf' (grave owned by the church) somewhere under a tombstone in the church. After twenty years, his remains were taken away and destroyed, as was customary with the remains of poor people at the time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_paintings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of paintings by Rembrandt">List of paintings by Rembrandt</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_etchings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of etchings by Rembrandt">List of etchings by Rembrandt</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_drawings_by_Rembrandt" title="List of drawings by Rembrandt">List of drawings by Rembrandt</a></div>
<p>In a letter to Huygens, Rembrandt offered the only surviving explanation of what he sought to achieve through his art: <i>the greatest and most natural movement</i>, translated from <i>de meeste en de natuurlijkste beweegelijkheid</i>. The word "beweegelijkheid" is also argued to mean "emotion" or "motive". Whether this refers to objectives, material or otherwise, is open to interpretation; either way, critics have drawn particular attention to the way Rembrandt seamlessly melded the earthly and spiritual.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg" width="220" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1930" data-file-height="2400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Christ_in_the_Storm_on_the_Lake_of_Galilee.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Storm_on_the_Sea_of_Galilee" title="The Storm on the Sea of Galilee">The Storm on the Sea of Galilee</a></i>, 1633. The painting is still missing after the <a href="/wiki/Isabella_Stewart_Gardner_Museum_theft" title="Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum theft">robbery from the Isabella Stewart Gardner Museum</a> in 1990.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earlier 20th century connoisseurs claimed Rembrandt had produced well over 600 paintings,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> nearly 400 <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a> and 2,000 drawings.<sup id="cite_ref-Rembrandtart_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rembrandtart-39">[39]</a></sup> More recent scholarship, from the 1960s to the present day (led by the <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_Research_Project" title="Rembrandt Research Project">Rembrandt Research Project</a>), often controversially, has winnowed his oeuvre to nearer 300 paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Old_master_print" title="Old master print">prints</a>, traditionally all called <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a>, although many are produced in whole or part by <a href="/wiki/Engraving" title="Engraving">engraving</a> and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Drypoint" title="Drypoint">drypoint</a>, have a much more stable total of slightly under&#160;300.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> It is likely Rembrandt made many more drawings in his lifetime than 2,000, but those extant are more rare than presumed.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Two experts claim that the number of drawings whose autograph status can be regarded as effectively "certain" is no higher than about&#160;75, although this is disputed. The list was to be unveiled at a scholarly meeting in February&#160;2010.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>At one time about ninety paintings were counted as <a href="/wiki/Self-portraits_by_Rembrandt" title="Self-portraits by Rembrandt">Rembrandt self-portraits</a>, but it is now known that he had his students copy his own self-portraits as part of their training. Modern scholarship has reduced the autograph count to over forty paintings, as well as a few drawings and thirty-one etchings, which include many of the most remarkable images of the group.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Some show him posing in quasi-historical fancy dress, or pulling faces at himself. His oil paintings trace the progress from an uncertain young man, through the dapper and very successful portrait-painter of the 1630s, to the troubled but massively powerful portraits of his old age. Together they give a remarkably clear picture of the man, his appearance and his psychological make-up, as revealed by his richly weathered face.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg" width="220" height="323" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3259" data-file-height="4781" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_van_Rijn_-_A_Polish_nobleman.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/A_Polish_Nobleman" title="A Polish Nobleman">A Polish Nobleman</a></i>, 1637</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In his portraits and self-portraits, he angles the sitter's face in such a way that the ridge of the nose nearly always forms the line of demarcation between brightly illuminated and shadowy areas. A Rembrandt face is a face partially eclipsed; and the nose, bright and obvious, thrusting into the riddle of halftones, serves to focus the viewer's attention upon, and to dramatize, the division between a flood of light—an overwhelming clarity—and a brooding duskiness.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a number of biblical works, including <i>The Raising of the Cross</i>, <i>Joseph Telling His Dreams</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stoning_of_Saint_Stephen" title="The Stoning of Saint Stephen">The Stoning of Saint Stephen</a></i>, Rembrandt painted himself as a character in the crowd. Durham suggests that this was because the Bible was for Rembrandt "a kind of diary, an account of moments in his own life".<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the more prominent characteristics of Rembrandt's work are his use of <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a>, the theatrical employment of light and shadow derived from <a href="/wiki/Caravaggio" title="Caravaggio">Caravaggio</a>, or, more likely, from the Dutch <a href="/wiki/Utrecht_Caravaggism" title="Utrecht Caravaggism">Caravaggisti</a>, but adapted for very personal means.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Also notable are his dramatic and lively presentation of subjects, devoid of the rigid formality that his contemporaries often displayed, and a deeply felt compassion for mankind, irrespective of wealth and age. His immediate family—his wife Saskia, his son Titus and his common-law wife Hendrickje—often figured prominently in his paintings, many of which had <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythical</a>, biblical or historical themes.</p>
<p>Drawings by Rembrandt and his pupils have been extensively studied by many artists and scholars<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> through the centuries. His original draughtsmanship has been described as an individualistic art style that was very similar to East Asian old masters, most notably Chinese masters:<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> a "combination of formal clarity and <a href="/wiki/Calligraphy" title="Calligraphy">calligraphic</a> vitality in the movement of pen or brush that is closer to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese painting</a> in technique and feeling than to anything in European art before the twentieth century".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Periods,_themes_and_styles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Periods.2C_themes_and_styles">Periods, themes and styles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Periods, themes and styles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout his career Rembrandt took as his primary subjects the themes of portraiture, landscape and narrative painting. For the last, he was especially praised by his contemporaries, who extolled him as a masterly interpreter of biblical stories for his skill in representing emotions and attention to detail.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Stylistically, his paintings progressed from the early "smooth" manner, characterized by fine technique in the portrayal of illusionistic form, to the late "rough" treatment of richly variegated paint surfaces, which allowed for an illusionism of form suggested by the tactile quality of the paint itself.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="30000" data-file-height="23413" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_-_The_Abduction_of_Europa_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/The_Abduction_of_Europa_(Rembrandt)" title="The Abduction of Europa (Rembrandt)"><i>The Abduction of Europa</i></a>, 1632. Oil on panel. The work has been described as "...a shining example of the 'golden age' of <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">Baroque</a> painting".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A parallel development may be seen in Rembrandt's skill as a printmaker. In the etchings of his maturity, particularly from the late 1640s onward, the freedom and breadth of his drawings and paintings found expression in the print medium as well. The works encompass a wide range of subject matter and technique, sometimes leaving large areas of white paper to suggest space, at other times employing complex webs of line to produce rich dark tones.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was during Rembrandt's Leiden period (1625–1631) that Lastman's influence was most prominent. It is also likely that at this time Lievens had a strong impact on his work as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-56">[56]</a></sup> Paintings were rather small, but rich in details (for example, in costumes and jewelry). Religious and <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegorical</a> themes were favored, as were <a href="/wiki/Tronie" title="Tronie">tronies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1626 Rembrandt produced his first etchings, the wide dissemination of which would largely account for his international fame.<sup id="cite_ref-Wetering-284_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wetering-284-56">[56]</a></sup> In 1629 he completed <i><a href="/wiki/Judas_Repentant,_Returning_the_Pieces_of_Silver" title="Judas Repentant, Returning the Pieces of Silver">Judas Repentant, Returning the Pieces of Silver</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Artist_in_his_studio" class="mw-redirect" title="Artist in his studio">The Artist in His Studio</a></i>, works that evidence his interest in the handling of light and variety of paint application, and constitute the first major progress in his development as a painter.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg/170px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg/255px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg/340px-Rembrandt%2C_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3199" data-file-height="4319" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_Portret_van_Haesje_v.Cleyburg_1634_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A typical portrait from 1634, when Rembrandt was enjoying great commercial success</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During his early years in Amsterdam (1632–1636), Rembrandt began to paint dramatic biblical and mythological scenes in high contrast and of large format (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Blinding_of_Samson" title="The Blinding of Samson">The Blinding of Samson</a></i>, 1636, <i><a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Rembrandt)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Rembrandt)">Belshazzar's Feast</a></i>, c.&#160;1635 <i><a href="/wiki/Dana%C3%AB_(Rembrandt)" class="mw-redirect" title="Danaë (Rembrandt)">Danaë</a></i>, 1636), seeking to emulate the baroque style of <a href="/wiki/Rubens" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubens">Rubens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> With the occasional help of assistants in Uylenburgh's workshop, he painted numerous portrait commissions both small (<a href="/wiki/Jacob_de_Gheyn_III_(painting)" class="mw-redirect" title="Jacob de Gheyn III (painting)"><i>Jacob de Gheyn&#160;III</i></a>) and large (<i>Portrait of the Shipbuilder Jan Rijcksen and his Wife</i>, 1633, <i><a href="/wiki/Anatomy_Lesson_of_Dr._Nicolaes_Tulp" class="mw-redirect" title="Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp">Anatomy Lesson of Dr. Nicolaes Tulp</a></i>, 1632).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the late 1630s Rembrandt had produced a few paintings and many etchings of <a href="/wiki/Landscape_painting" title="Landscape painting">landscapes</a>. Often these landscapes highlighted natural drama, featuring uprooted trees and ominous skies (<i>Cottages before a Stormy Sky</i>, c.&#160;1641; <i>The Three Trees</i>, 1643). From 1640 his work became less exuberant and more sober in tone, possibly reflecting personal tragedy. Biblical scenes were now derived more often from the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, as had been the case before. In 1642 he painted <i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i>, the most substantial of the important group portrait commissions which he received in this period, and through which he sought to find solutions to compositional and narrative problems that had been attempted in previous works.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5009" data-file-height="6387" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Zelfportret_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Self Portrait</i>, 1658, <a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a>, a masterpiece of the final style, "the calmest and grandest of all his portraits"<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the decade following the <i>Night Watch</i>, Rembrandt's paintings varied greatly in size, subject, and style. The previous tendency to create dramatic effects primarily by strong contrasts of light and shadow gave way to the use of frontal lighting and larger and more saturated areas of color. Simultaneously, figures came to be placed parallel to the picture plane. These changes can be seen as a move toward a classical mode of composition and, considering the more expressive use of brushwork as well, may indicate a familiarity with Venetian art (<i>Susanna and the Elders</i>, 1637–47).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> At the same time, there was a marked decrease in painted works in favor of etchings and drawings of landscapes.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In these graphic works natural drama eventually made way for quiet Dutch rural scenes.</p>
<p>In the 1650s, Rembrandt's style changed again. Colors became richer and brush strokes more pronounced. With these changes, Rembrandt distanced himself from earlier work and current fashion, which increasingly inclined toward fine, detailed works. His use of light becomes more jagged and harsh, and shine becomes almost nonexistent. His singular approach to paint application may have been suggested in part by familiarity with the work of <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>, and could be seen in the context of the then current discussion of 'finish' and surface quality of paintings. Contemporary accounts sometimes remark disapprovingly of the coarseness of Rembrandt's brushwork, and the artist himself was said to have dissuaded visitors from looking too closely at his paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The tactile manipulation of paint may hearken to medieval procedures, when mimetic effects of rendering informed a painting's surface. The end result is a richly varied handling of paint, deeply layered and often apparently haphazard, which suggests form and space in both an illusory and highly individual manner.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In later years biblical themes were still depicted often, but emphasis shifted from dramatic group scenes to intimate portrait-like figures (<i>James the Apostle</i>, 1661). In his last years, Rembrandt painted his most deeply reflective self-portraits (from 1652 to 1669 he painted fifteen), and several moving images of both men and women (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_Bride" title="The Jewish Bride">The Jewish Bride</a></i>, c.&#160;1666)—in love, in life, and before God.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etchings">Etchings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Etchings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4122" data-file-height="2869" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_The_windmill_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Windmill, <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rembrandt produced <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etchings</a> for most of his career, from 1626 to 1660, when he was forced to sell his printing-press and practically abandoned etching. Only the troubled year of 1649 produced no dated work.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> He took easily to etching and, though he also learned to use a <a href="/wiki/Burin_(engraving)" title="Burin (engraving)">burin</a> and partly <a href="/wiki/Engraving" title="Engraving">engraved</a> many plates, the freedom of etching technique was fundamental to his work. He was very closely involved in the whole process of printmaking, and must have printed at least early examples of his etchings himself. At first he used a style based on drawing, but soon moved to one based on painting, using a mass of lines and numerous bitings with the acid to achieve different strengths of line. Towards the end of the 1630s, he reacted against this manner and moved to a simpler style, with fewer bitings.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> He worked on the so-called <i><a href="/wiki/Hundred_Guilder_Print" title="Hundred Guilder Print">Hundred Guilder Print</a></i> in stages throughout the 1640s, and it was the "critical work in the middle of his career", from which his final etching style began to emerge.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Although the print only survives in two <a href="/wiki/State_(printmaking)" title="State (printmaking)">states</a>, the first very rare, evidence of much reworking can be seen underneath the final print and many drawings survive for elements of it.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/220px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/330px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg/440px-Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Die_landschaft_mit_den_drei_baeumen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The three trees, 1643, etching</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the mature works of the 1650s, Rembrandt was more ready to improvise on the plate and large prints typically survive in several states, up to eleven, often radically changed. He now uses <a href="/wiki/Hatching" title="Hatching">hatching</a> to create his dark areas, which often take up much of the plate. He also experimented with the effects of printing on different kinds of paper, including Japanese paper, which he used frequently, and on <a href="/wiki/Vellum" title="Vellum">vellum</a>. He began to use "surface tone," leaving a thin film of ink on parts of the plate instead of wiping it completely clean to print each impression. He made more use of <a href="/wiki/Drypoint" title="Drypoint">drypoint</a>, exploiting, especially in landscapes, the rich fuzzy burr that this technique gives to the first few impressions.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>His prints have similar subjects to his paintings, although the twenty-seven self-portraits are relatively more common, and portraits of other people less so. There are forty-six landscapes, mostly small, which largely set the course for the graphic treatment of landscape until the end of the 19th century. One third of his etchings are of religious subjects, many treated with a homely simplicity, whilst others are his most monumental prints. A few erotic, or just obscene, compositions have no equivalent in his paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> He owned, until forced to sell it, a magnificent collection of prints by other artists, and many borrowings and influences in his work can be traced to artists as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Andrea_Mantegna" title="Andrea Mantegna">Mantegna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hercules_Seghers" title="Hercules Seghers">Hercules Seghers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Benedetto_Castiglione" title="Giovanni Benedetto Castiglione">Giovanni Benedetto Castiglione</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Night_Watch"><i>The Night Watch</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The Night Watch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg/300px-The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg" width="300" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg/450px-The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg/600px-The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="14168" data-file-height="11528" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Nightwatch_by_Rembrandt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch">The Night Watch</a></i> or <i>The Militia Company of Captain Frans Banning Cocq</i>, 1642. Oil on canvas; on display at the <a href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum_Amsterdam" class="mw-redirect" title="Rijksmuseum Amsterdam">Rijksmuseum</a>, Amsterdam</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Rembrandt painted the large painting <i>The Militia Company of Captain Frans Banning Cocq</i> between 1640 and 1642. This picture was called <i>De Nachtwacht</i> by the Dutch and <i>The Night Watch</i> by Sir <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">Joshua Reynolds</a> because by the 18th century the picture was so dimmed and defaced that it was almost indistinguishable, and it looked quite like a night scene. After it was cleaned, it was discovered to represent broad day—a party of <a href="/wiki/Musketeer" title="Musketeer">musketeers</a> stepping from a gloomy courtyard into the blinding sunlight.</p>
<p>The piece was commissioned for the new hall of the <i><a href="/wiki/Kloveniersdoelen,_Amsterdam" title="Kloveniersdoelen, Amsterdam">Kloveniersdoelen</a></i>, the musketeer branch of the civic militia. Rembrandt departed from convention, which ordered that such genre pieces should be stately and formal, rather a line-up than an action scene. Instead he showed the militia readying themselves to embark on a mission (what kind of mission, an ordinary patrol or some special event, is a matter of debate).</p>
<p>Contrary to what is often said, the work was hailed as a success from the beginning. Parts of the canvas were cut off (approximately 20% from the left hand side was removed) to make the painting fit its new position when it was moved to <a href="/wiki/Royal_Palace_of_Amsterdam" title="Royal Palace of Amsterdam">Amsterdam town hall</a> in 1715; the Rijksmuseum has a smaller copy of what is thought to be the full original composition; the four figures in the front are at the centre of the canvas. The painting is now in the <a href="/wiki/Rijksmuseum" title="Rijksmuseum">Rijksmuseum</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expert_assessments">Expert assessments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Expert assessments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_catalog_raisonn%C3%A9,_1968" title="Rembrandt catalog raisonné, 1968">Rembrandt catalog raisonné, 1968</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter,_c.1655_(Frick_Collection).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter%2C_c.1655_%28Frick_Collection%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5832" data-file-height="5071" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_De_Poolse_ruiter,_c.1655_(Frick_Collection).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Polish_Rider" title="The Polish Rider">The Polish Rider</a></i> – Possibly a <a href="/wiki/Lisowczycy" title="Lisowczycy">Lisowczyk</a> on horseback.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1968 the Rembrandt Research Project began under the sponsorship of the Netherlands Organization for the Advancement of Scientific Research; it was initially expected to last a highly optimistic ten years. Art historians teamed up with experts from other fields to reassess the authenticity of works attributed to Rembrandt, using all methods available, including state-of-the-art technical diagnostics, and to compile a complete new <a href="/wiki/Catalogue_raisonn%C3%A9" title="Catalogue raisonné">catalogue raisonné</a> of his paintings. As a result of their findings, many paintings that were previously attributed to Rembrandt have been removed from their list, although others have been added back.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Many of those removed are now thought to be the work of his students.</p>
<p>One example of activity is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Polish_Rider" title="The Polish Rider">The Polish Rider</a></i>, in New York's <a href="/wiki/Frick_Collection" title="Frick Collection">Frick Collection</a>. Rembrandt's authorship had been questioned by at least one scholar, Alfred von Wurzbach, at the beginning of the twentieth century, but for many decades later most scholars, including the foremost authority writing in English, <a href="/wiki/Julius_S._Held" title="Julius S. Held">Julius S. Held</a>, agreed that it was indeed by the master. In the 1980s, however, Dr.&#160;Josua Bruyn of the Foundation Rembrandt Research Project cautiously and tentatively attributed the painting to one of Rembrandt's closest and most talented pupils, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Drost" title="Willem Drost">Willem Drost</a>, about whom little is known. But Bruyn's remained a minority opinion, the suggestion of Drost's authorship is now generally rejected, and the Frick itself never changed its own attribution, the label still reading "Rembrandt" and not "attributed to" or "school of". More recent opinion has shifted even more decisively in favor of the Frick, with <a href="/wiki/Simon_Schama" title="Simon Schama">Simon Schama</a> (in his 1999 book <i>Rembrandt's Eyes</i>) and the Rembrandt Project scholar Ernst van de Wetering (Melbourne Symposium, 1997) both arguing for attribution to the master. Those few scholars who still question Rembrandt's authorship feel that the execution is uneven, and favour different attributions for different parts of the work.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/220px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/330px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg/440px-Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1204" data-file-height="1598" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mann_mit_dem_Goldhelm.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_with_the_Golden_Helmet" title="The Man with the Golden Helmet">The Man with the Golden Helmet</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie,_Berlin" title="Gemäldegalerie, Berlin">Gemäldegalerie, Berlin</a>, once one of the most famous "Rembrandt" portraits, is no longer attributed to the master.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A similar issue was raised by Simon Schama in his book <i>Rembrandt's Eyes</i> concerning the verification of titles associated with the subject matter depicted in Rembrandt's works. For example, the exact subject being portrayed in <i><a href="/wiki/Aristotle_with_a_Bust_of_Homer" title="Aristotle with a Bust of Homer">Aristotle with a Bust of Homer</a></i> (recently retitled by curators at the Metropolitan Museum) has been directly challenged by Schama applying the scholarship of Paul Crenshaw.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Schama presents a substantial argument that it was the famous ancient Greek painter <a href="/wiki/Apelles" title="Apelles">Apelles</a> who is depicted in contemplation by Rembrandt and not Aristotle.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another painting, <i>Pilate Washing His Hands</i>, is also of questionable attribution. Critical opinion of this picture has varied since 1905, when Wilhelm von Bode described it as "a somewhat abnormal work" by Rembrandt. Scholars have since dated the painting to the 1660s and assigned it to an anonymous pupil, possibly Aert de Gelder. The composition bears superficial resemblance to mature works by Rembrandt but lacks the master's command of illumination and modeling.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The attribution and re-attribution work is ongoing. In 2005 four oil paintings previously attributed to Rembrandt's students were reclassified as the work of Rembrandt himself: <i>Study of an Old Man in Profile</i> and <i>Study of an Old Man with a Beard</i> from a US private collection, <i>Study of a Weeping Woman</i>, owned by the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Institute_of_Arts" title="Detroit Institute of Arts">Detroit Institute of Arts</a>, and <i>Portrait of an Elderly Woman in a White Bonnet</i>, painted in 1640.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The <i>Old Man Sitting in a Chair</i> is a further example: in 2014, Professor <a href="/wiki/Ernst_van_de_Wetering" title="Ernst van de Wetering">Ernst van de Wetering</a> offered his view to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> that the demotion of the 1652 painting <i>Old Man Sitting in a Chair</i> "was a vast mistake...it is a most important painting. The painting needs to be seen in terms of Rembrandt’s experimentation”. This was highlighted much earlier by <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Konstam" title="Nigel Konstam">Nigel Konstam</a> who studied Rembrandt throughout his career.</p>
<p>Rembrandt's own studio practice is a major factor in the difficulty of attribution, since, like many masters before him, he encouraged his students to copy his paintings, sometimes finishing or retouching them to be sold as originals, and sometimes selling them as authorized copies. Additionally, his style proved easy enough for his most talented students to emulate. Further complicating matters is the uneven quality of some of Rembrandt's own work, and his frequent stylistic evolutions and experiments.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> As well, there were later imitations of his work, and restorations which so seriously damaged the original works that they are no longer recognizable.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It is highly likely that there will never be universal agreement as to what does and what does not constitute a genuine Rembrandt.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Painting_materials">Painting materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Painting materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/220px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg/440px-Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg 2x" data-file-width="776" data-file-height="1010" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_-_Saskia_van_Uylenburgh_in_Arcadian_Costume_-_WGA19164.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Saskia as Flora</i>, 1635</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Technical investigation of Rembrandt's paintings in the possession of the <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister</a><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> and in the <a href="/wiki/Gem%C3%A4ldegalerie_Alte_Meister_(Kassel)" title="Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister (Kassel)">Gemäldegalerie Alte Meister (Kassel)</a><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> has been conducted by Hermann Kühn in 1977. The pigment analyses of some thirty paintings have shown that Rembrandt's palette consisted of the following pigments: <a href="/wiki/Lead_white" class="mw-redirect" title="Lead white">lead white</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Ochres" class="mw-redirect" title="Ochres">ochres</a>, Vandyke brown, <a href="/wiki/Bone_black" class="mw-redirect" title="Bone black">bone black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charcoal_black" class="mw-redirect" title="Charcoal black">charcoal black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamp_black" class="mw-redirect" title="Lamp black">lamp black</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermilion" title="Vermilion">vermilion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madder_lake" class="mw-redirect" title="Madder lake">madder lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultramarine" title="Ultramarine">ultramarine</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yellow_lake&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Yellow lake (page does not exist)">yellow lake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lead-tin-yellow" title="Lead-tin-yellow">lead-tin-yellow</a>. One painting (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://skd-online-collection.skd.museum/de/contents/showSearch?id=376271">Saskia van Uylenburgh as Flora</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> reportedly contains <a href="/wiki/Gamboge" title="Gamboge">gamboge</a>. Rembrandt very rarely used pure blue or green colors, the most pronounced exception being <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar%27s_Feast_(Rembrandt)" title="Belshazzar's Feast (Rembrandt)">Belshazzar's Feast</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_in_London" class="mw-redirect" title="National Gallery in London">National Gallery in London</a>. The book by Bomford (above reference) describes more recent technical investigations and pigment analyses of Rembrandt's paintings predominantly in the National Gallery in London. The entire array of pigments employed by Rembrandt can be found at ColourLex.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The best source for technical information on Rembrandt's paintings on the web is the Rembrandt Database containing all works of Rembrandt with detailed investigative reports, infrared and radiography images and other scientific details.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_signature">Name and signature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Name and signature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_bue_squartato,_1655,_02.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/170px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/255px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG/340px-Rembrandt%2C_bue_squartato%2C_1655%2C_02.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2736" data-file-height="3648" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt,_bue_squartato,_1655,_02.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Slaughtered_Ox" title="Slaughtered Ox">Slaughtered Ox</a></i>, (1655), <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre" class="mw-redirect" title="Musée du Louvre">Musée du Louvre</a>, Paris</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"<b>Rembrandt</b>" is a modification of the spelling of the artist's first name that he introduced in 1633. Roughly speaking, his earliest signatures (ca. 1625) consisted of an initial&#160;"<b>R</b>", or the monogram "<b>RH</b>" (for Rembrant Harmenszoon; i.e.&#160;"Rembrant, the son of Harmen"), and starting in 1629, "<b>RHL</b>" (the "L" stood, presumably, for Leiden). In 1632, he used this monogram early in the year, then added his family name to it, "<b>RHL-van Rijn</b>", but replaced this form in that same year and began using his first name alone with its original spelling, "<b>Rembrant</b>". In 1633 he added&#160;a&#160;"d", and maintained this form consistently from then on, proving that this minor change had a meaning for him (whatever it might have been). This change is purely visual; it does not change the way his name is pronounced. Curiously enough, despite the large number of paintings and etchings signed with this modified first name, most of their documents that mentioned him during his lifetime retained the original "Rembrant" spelling. (Note: the rough chronology of signature forms above applies to the paintings, and to a lesser degree to the etchings; from 1632, presumably, there is only one etching signed "RHL-v. Rijn," the large-format "Raising of Lazarus,"&#160;B&#160;73).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> His practice of signing his work with his first name, later followed by <a href="/wiki/Vincent_van_Gogh" title="Vincent van Gogh">Vincent van Gogh</a>, was probably inspired by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> who, then as now, were referred to by their first names alone.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Workshop">Workshop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Workshop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Rembrandt ran a large workshop and had many pupils. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_Rembrandt_pupils" title="List of Rembrandt pupils">list of Rembrandt pupils</a> from his period in Leiden as well as his time in Amsterdam is quite long, mostly because his influence on painters around him was so great that it is difficult to tell whether someone worked for him in his studio or just copied his style for patrons eager to acquire a Rembrandt. A partial list should include<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Bol" title="Ferdinand Bol">Ferdinand Bol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adriaen_Brouwer" title="Adriaen Brouwer">Adriaen Brouwer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerrit_Dou" title="Gerrit Dou">Gerrit Dou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willem_Drost" title="Willem Drost">Willem Drost</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heiman_Dullaart" title="Heiman Dullaart">Heiman Dullaart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerbrand_van_den_Eeckhout" title="Gerbrand van den Eeckhout">Gerbrand van den Eeckhout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carel_Fabritius" title="Carel Fabritius">Carel Fabritius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Govert_Flinck" title="Govert Flinck">Govert Flinck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hendrick_Fromantiou" title="Hendrick Fromantiou">Hendrick Fromantiou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aert_de_Gelder" title="Aert de Gelder">Aert de Gelder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Dirksz_van_Hoogstraten" title="Samuel Dirksz van Hoogstraten">Samuel Dirksz van Hoogstraten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Janssens" title="Abraham Janssens">Abraham Janssens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_de_Koninck" title="Philip de Koninck">Philip de Koninck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Levecq" title="Jacob Levecq">Jacob Levecq</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicolaes_Maes" title="Nicolaes Maes">Nicolaes Maes</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C3%BCrgen_Ovens" title="Jürgen Ovens">Jürgen Ovens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Paudi%C3%9F" title="Christopher Paudiß">Christopher Paudiß</a>, <a href="/wiki/Willem_de_Poorter" title="Willem de Poorter">Willem de Poorter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Victors" title="Jan Victors">Jan Victors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Willem_van_der_Vliet" title="Willem van der Vliet">Willem van der Vliet</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Museum_collections">Museum collections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Rembrandt&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Museum collections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandts_house,_Amsterdam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/170px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/255px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg/340px-Rembrandts_house%2C_Amsterdam.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rembrandts_house,_Amsterdam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rembrandt_House_Museum" title="Rembrandt House Museum">Rembrandt House Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
